\begin{itemize}

\[

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Thus, the game is more complex. Alice's moves increase sum and squares; Bazza's moves also increase sum and squares, but he must keep squares within bound. He can try to increase sum to make it impossible for Alice to continue, or he can try to keep squares low to prevent Alice from forcing a win.

We need to analyze the game as an infinite game with perfect information. This is reminiscent of a "game of balancing" where players choose numbers with constraints. 4x1+2x2-6λ = 2μ x1  (1)
4x2+2x1-6λ = 2μ x2  (2)
x1^2+x2^2=2.

Subtract (1) and (2): (4x1+2x2-6λ) - (4x2+2x1-6λ) = 2μ(x1-x2) => (4x1+2x2-4x2-2x1)=2μ(x1-x2) => 2x1-2x2=2μ(x1-x2) => 2(x1-x2)=2μ(x1-x2) => (x1-x2)(1-μ)=0. So either x1=x2, or μ=1.

So the game reduces to a kind of "threshold" analysis. Let's explore the function f(x1,x2) = x1^2+x2^2+(3λ - x1 - x2)^2. But maybe she can adopt a strategy that eventually forces T_n to exceed n+1. Let's try to see if for λ between √2/3 and 1/√2, Bazza can always keep T_n ≤ n+1? That is, he can prevent her from winning. We need to analyze the game more systematically.

The game is an infinite game with constraints. Often, such problems are solved by considering "strategy stealing" or by comparing the maximum possible sum of squares per index. Since Alice's goal is to achieve T_n > n+1, she can try to make T as large as possible given the sum constraint. Bazza's goal is to keep T ≤ n+1. We can think of it as a kind of "balanced" game where the players choose numbers with constraints on the cumulative sum and sum of squares. The game can be analyzed by looking at the "potential" function Φ(n) = T_n - n. If at any odd n, T_n > n+1, then she wins. So she wants to push Φ above 1.

Alternatively, we can analyze the game by considering the maximum possible T_n after n turns given optimal play by both. Since Alice wants to maximize T_n, and Bazza wants to minimize T_n (or at least keep it below the threshold), we can compute the "value" of the game as a function of λ. This is reminiscent of a "game of balancing" where the constraint for Alice is on sum, and for Bazza on squares. Usually, such games have a threshold λ = 2? But we already have Alice winning for λ≥1? We need to determine the exact set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. Let's try to analyze the game more systematically.

Define the game as an infinite game. The players choose numbers with constraints. The game ends when a player cannot move. The problem asks: For which λ does Alice have a winning strategy? That is, she can force a win. For which λ does Bazza have a winning strategy?

We can think of it as a "positional" game. The state can be represented by the current sum S and sum of squares T, and whose turn it is. Since the constraints depend on parity, the state also includes the index n (which is determined by the number of moves made). However, n is known to both players. So the state is (n, S, T). The players must choose x≥0 such that S+x ≤ λ n (if n odd) or T+y^2 ≤ n (if n even). The game ends if no such x exists.

We want to know for which λ the first player (Alice) has a winning strategy.

Let's denote the set of positions where the player to move can force a win. This is a typical impartial game? Not exactly impartial because the constraints differ based on parity, but the game is two-player with alternating moves. We can attempt to analyze by considering the "critical" conditions.

Thus, the game reduces to: Alice chooses x1; then Bazza chooses y1 with y1^2 ≤ 2 - x1^2; then Alice chooses x3 with x3 ≤ 3λ - (x1+y1); then Bazza chooses y3 with y3^2 ≤ 4 - T3; and so on. This is a game where both players can adapt. We can try to find the "critical" λ by analyzing the "balanced" strategy where both play optimally to keep the game going.

Observation: The constraints are linear in sum and quadratic in squares. The game is similar to a "shooting" game where Alice tries to make T_n exceed n+1, while Bazza tries to keep it below. Since Bazza can always set y=0, the game reduces to Alice's choices only, and she can try to force T to exceed the index by some turn. But Bazza can also choose y>0 to increase T2, which might help Alice? Actually, increasing T2 would make T3 larger, which is good for her, but Bazza would not do that because he wants to prevent her from winning. So he will set y to minimize T3, given x1. So for a given x1, the best response for Bazza is to choose y = min(√(2-x1^2), something that minimizes T3? But T3 = x1^2+y^2+(3λ - x1 - y)^2. This is a quadratic in y. The unconstrained minimum is at y=1.5λ, but that may not be feasible because y must satisfy y^2 ≤ 2-x1^2. Also y≥0. So the optimal y for Bazza is to choose y that minimizes T3 subject to y ∈ [0, √(2-x1^2)]. Since the quadratic is convex, the minimum occurs at the point closest to the vertex. So if the vertex y=1.5λ is within the feasible range, then he can set y=1.5λ, giving T3 = 2*(1.5λ)^2 -6λ*(1.5λ)+9λ^2 = 4.5λ^2. So he will set y = √(2-x1^2). If 1.5λ ≤ √, then the vertex is within the range, so he sets y = 1.5λ.

Thus, Bazza's best response given x1 is:

- If √(2-x1^2) ≥ 1.5λ, then he sets y = 1.5λ, and T3 = 4.5λ^2.
- If √(2-x1^2) < 1.5λ, then he sets y = √(2-x1^2), and T3 = x1^2 + (2-x1^2) + (3λ - x1 - √(2-x1^2))^2? Actually, T3 = x1^2 + y^2 + (3λ - x1 - y)^2 with y=√(2-x1^2). Now, note that this best response is independent of the index? Actually, it's for the second move. Then the game continues.

We can attempt to find the maximum possible T after each odd turn given that Bazza plays optimally. This is a kind of "dynamic programming" problem. Since the game is deterministic, we can compute the "value" V_k = the maximum possible T after k odd turns (i.e., after Alice's move at turn 2k+1) given that both play optimally, assuming that Bazza tries to keep T low. But Alice wants to maximize T, so she will choose x to increase T as much as possible. However, Bazza will then choose y to minimize T on the next even turn. So the evolution is:

Let S_n be the sum after the previous turn. At odd turn n (n=2k+1), Alice chooses x_n ≥0 such that S_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n. She can choose any x_n. After her move, T_n = T_{n-1} + x_n^2. Then Bazza's turn (n+1 even), he chooses y_n ≥0 such that T_n + y_n^2 ≤ n+1. He will choose y_n to minimize T_{n+1}? Actually, he wants to prevent her from winning, so he will choose y_n to minimize the future T, but he also may want to keep the sum S low? Actually, his choice of y_n will affect S_{n+1} = S_n + y_n, and that will affect future Alice moves. So it's a game with state (S, T, n). We need to analyze the game to determine for which λ Alice can force a win.

It seems that for λ=0.72, Bazza can always force a win by making S at the start of her turn exceed λ n. Let's try to find if there is any λ for which she can avoid that. The condition for her to survive at turn n (odd) is that S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. After her move at turn n-2 (odd), we have S_{n-2}=λ (n-2). Then Bazza's turn (n-1 even), he chooses y_{n-1} with y_{n-1}^2 ≤ (n-1) - T_{n-2} (since T_{n-1} = T_{n-2}+y_{n-1}^2 ≤ n-1). He can increase S_{n-1} by y_{n-1}. To cause her loss at turn n, he needs S_{n-1} > λ n, i.e., λ (n-2) + y_{n-1} > λ n => y_{n-1} > 2λ. So if he can choose y_{n-1} > 2λ, then she loses. But he must also have y_{n-1}^2 ≤ (n-1) - T_{n-2}. So if (n-1) - T_{n-2} is large enough to allow y_{n-1} > 2λ, then he can. So the question reduces to: Can Bazza, at some even turn, make his y large enough to exceed 2λ, given the state? If he can, he wins. If not, Alice can continue.

Thus, Alice wants to ensure that for all even n, the maximum possible y she can play (given T_{n-1}) is ≤ 2λ, so that Bazza cannot increase sum beyond λ n. She can influence T_{n-1} by her choices. So she might try to keep T_{n-1} large enough that the bound (n-1) - T_{n-1} is small, limiting y. Actually, the maximum y is sqrt((n-1) - T_{n-1})? Wait, on turn n (even), the constraint is T_n ≤ n, so T_{n-1} + y^2 ≤ n => y ≤ sqrt(n - T_{n-1}). So if T_{n-1} is close to n, then y is small. So if she can make T_{n-1} large, she limits his ability to increase sum. So she wants to maximize T quickly, but she also must respect sum bounds. However, if she tries to increase T, she might be limited by sum. So the game is about the trade-off.

This is reminiscent of a "balancing" game where the threshold λ is such that if λ > something, she can force T to be large enough to prevent him from causing sum overflow; if λ is smaller, he can cause overflow.

Let's try to find the exact critical λ by analyzing the game. The game seems to be about whether Alice can "save up" enough squares to eventually exceed the index. The condition for her to win is that there exists a sequence of moves such that she can force T_n > n+1 for some odd n. This is similar to a "pursuit" game where she tries to make T as large as possible, while he tries to keep it low. The key observation: The game can be analyzed by considering the "balance" condition that if λ is large enough, Alice can force the sum of squares to grow faster than the index, and Bazza cannot prevent it because he cannot increase the sum enough to cause her loss. We need to find the threshold λ* such that if λ > λ*, Alice has a winning strategy, else Bazza wins. From our simulations, λ* seems to be somewhere around 0.86? But we need to check λ=0.87.

Let's try to derive a condition by considering a "critical" strategy where both play optimally and the game continues indefinitely if λ is below some threshold. Perhaps the game is determined by the "infinite" game where both players try to keep the game going. The threshold λ might be the solution to an equation like λ^2 = something.

We can attempt to analyze the game using the concept of "potential function" P = T - n. Alice wins if she can make P > 1 at an odd turn. The players choose numbers. Consider the state after each even turn, say at the start of Alice's turn (odd n). At that point, we have S and T. She will choose x. The new T becomes T+x^2. Then Bazza's turn, he chooses y such that T+x^2+y^2 ≤ n+1. He wants to minimize T after his move? Actually, he wants to prevent her from winning, so he wants to keep T low. The maximum y he can choose is sqrt((n+1) - (T+x^2)). But he can also choose y=0. So after his move, T' = T+x^2+y^2, and S' = S+x+y. He will choose y to minimize T'? Actually, he wants to make it harder for her to later push T over the index. But his choice also affects S, which affects her ability to add squares. Since T is the sum of squares, and she wants it to be large, his increasing y increases T' (since y^2 adds to T). That would help her, so he will choose y=0 to keep T' as small as possible. But could setting y>0 ever help him? It might increase S, making her next x smaller, potentially reducing future squares. However, it also increases T, which is bad for him. So the trade-off is: if he sets y>0, he increases T (which is bad) and increases S (which may limit her future x). So the optimal for him is to set y=0 unless setting y>0 causes her to be unable to move on her next turn, which would be a win for him. So he can try to make S_{n+1} > λ (n+2) at the start of her next turn. This is the "sum overflow" strategy. So if at some even turn, he can choose y such that S_n + x + y > λ (n+2) (i.e., she cannot move at her next turn), he wins. So he will try to make S large enough to cause that.

Thus, the game is about whether he can ever make S exceed λ n at the start of her turn. He can only do that if the maximum y he can play is large enough to increase S beyond that bound. So we need to analyze the condition for him to be able to cause a sum overflow at some turn.

Suppose we are at the start of Alice's turn (odd n). Then S_{n-1} ≤ λ n (she can move). After her move, S_n = λ n (if she uses the full bound). Then Bazza's turn (n+1 even), he can choose y. He wants to make S_{n+1} = λ n + y > λ (n+2) (i.e., cause her loss at turn n+3). This requires y > 2λ. So if he can choose y > 2λ, he wins. The maximum y he can choose is sqrt((n+1) - T_n) (since T_{n+1} = T_n + y^2 ≤ n+1). So he can achieve y > 2λ if sqrt((n+1) - T_n) > 2λ, i.e., (n+1) - T_n > 4λ^2. So if T_n < n+1 - 4λ^2, then he can choose y = sqrt(n+1 - T_n) > 2λ, causing her loss. Thus, to avoid losing, Alice must ensure that at her turn (odd n), after her move, T_n ≥ n+1 - 4λ^2. Since she controls T_n, she can try to make T_n large enough to prevent him from overflowing. But she also must respect sum bounds. She can try to make T_n large by playing large x. So the condition for her to survive is that she can achieve T_n ≥ n+1 - 4λ^2 at some odd turn.

Now, note that T_n is the sum of squares after her move. At the start of her turn (odd n), she has T_{n-1} and S_{n-1}. She can choose x such that S_n = λ n (full sum) to maximize future possibilities. Then T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Since S_{n-1} could be less than λ (n-2) if Bazza played small y, but she might also have control over it. But to achieve a large T_n, she wants to add as much squares as possible. The maximum squares she can add on that turn is when S_{n-1} is as small as possible, so she can set x = λ n - S_{n-1} large. The smallest possible S_{n-1} is 0 (if she always plays 0 on odd turns? But then she doesn't increase T much). She can also try to keep S small by playing 0 on her first turn, then later she can add large numbers. But Bazza can also increase S by playing y. So the state evolves.

A systematic approach: Since the game is deterministic and perfect information, we can compute the "value" of the game by considering the "critical" λ as the solution to an equation where the "balance" of squares is exactly such that Bazza can just cause a sum overflow. The threshold λ might be the solution to λ^2 = 2? No.

Let's try to find the exact λ* by analyzing the optimal play for both. Suppose Alice wants to win. She can adopt the strategy: set x1 = 0. Then on each odd turn, she will choose the maximum possible x (i.e., set S_n = λ n) to make T_n as large as possible, given that Bazza will try to minimize T_n or cause overflow. We need to see if for some λ, this strategy leads to her winning eventually. For λ=0.9, we saw she won; for λ=0.85, we saw she lost. So we can try to find the boundary.

Let's derive the recurrence for T_n when both play optimally. Suppose we are at the start of Alice's turn (odd n). Let the state be (S, T). She will choose x = λ n - S (assuming S ≤ λ n). Then after her move, T' = T + (λ n - S)^2. Then Bazza's turn: he wants to maximize his chance of winning. He can choose y. He can either play y=0, leaving T' unchanged, or play y to increase S and T, possibly to cause Alice to lose on her next turn. He will choose y that minimizes her chance. The condition for him to win is to make S + x + y > λ (n+2) (i.e., she cannot move at her next odd turn). Since S + x = λ n, this is λ n + y > λ (n+2) => y > 2λ. So if he can choose y > 2λ, he wins. The maximum y he can choose is sqrt((n+1) - T') (since T'' = T' + y^2 ≤ n+1). So if sqrt((n+1) - T') > 2λ, i.e., T' < n+1 - 4λ^2, then he can choose y = sqrt(n+1 - T') (or any y > 2λ up to that bound) and cause her loss. If T' ≥ n+1 - 4λ^2, then he cannot cause immediate loss by overflow because even at maximum y, y = sqrt(n+1 - T') ≤ 2λ, so the sum becomes λ n + y ≤ λ n + 2λ = λ (n+2). So she can still move. In that case, he will choose y=0 to keep T' unchanged? Or he might choose some y to limit her future? But if he chooses y>0, T' increases, which is good for her, so he will choose y=0. So the best for him is to set y=0 if T' ≥ n+1 - 4λ^2. So the recurrence for T when both play optimally (Alice maximizing, Bazza minimizing) is:

At odd turn n (starting with n=1), let T_n be the sum of squares after Alice's move. Then:
- T_1 = x1^2. Alice chooses x1, with x1 ≤ λ. She can also choose 0. For her to have a chance, she might choose x1=0 to keep S small, but then she can add large squares later. But we need to see the optimal strategy.

We need to consider the game as a dynamic programming problem. Since the game is infinite, we can try to find the "critical" λ such that Alice can force a win if and only if λ > λ*. Let's try to analyze the "balanced" strategy where Alice always uses the maximum possible x on her turn, and Bazza always uses the maximum possible y on his turn (i.e., he tries to increase sum to cause overflow). This is a natural way to see if she can avoid losing.

We can consider a "potential" function: Define f(n) = T_n - n. She wins if f(n) > 1 at some odd n. The players choose numbers. For Alice, she wants to maximize f(n). For Bazza, he wants to minimize f(n) or cause her loss.

Let's attempt to find a condition on λ by assuming that Alice plays optimally and Bazza plays optimally to prevent her win. We can try to compute the maximum possible f(n) she can achieve after each odd turn, given that Bazza will respond optimally. This is like a two-player game where the state is (n, S, T). However, we can reduce the state by noting that after each even turn, Bazza can choose to set y to either 0 or to cause overflow. The game might be "determined" by a simple inequality.

We can consider the "critical" strategy where Alice uses the maximum x each turn, and Bazza uses the maximum y each turn (to increase sum to cause overflow). Then we can compute the evolution of T_n and S_n. The recurrence for T when both play optimally is:

Let n be odd. After Alice's move, T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Then Bazza's move: he will set y_n = sqrt((n+1) - T_n) if T_n ≤ n+1, otherwise he loses. But if T_n ≥ n+1, he loses. Then S_n = λ n. Then Bazza's turn: he will set y_{n+1} = sqrt((n+1) - T_n) (since T_n < n+1). Then S_{n+1} = λ n + y_{n+1}. Then T_{n+1} = n+1. So after each pair of turns (odd then even), T becomes the next even number, and S becomes λ n + sqrt((n+1) - T_n). But note that after Bazza's move, T = n+1, which is an even number. So at the start of the next odd turn (n+2), the state is (S_{n+1}, T_{n+1}=n+1). Then Alice will play x_{n+2} = λ (n+2) - S_{n+1}. So the recurrence for S and T is:

Given odd n, T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. But we also have that after Bazza's move, T_{n+1}=n+1. So we can set up a recurrence for S_n.

Let’s denote after Alice's move at odd n, we have T_n = T_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. Then after Bazza's move, T_{n+1}=n+1. So the next odd turn is n+2. The state at the start of turn n+2 is S_{n+1} = λ n + y_{n+1} = λ n + sqrt((n+1) - T_n). So S_{n+1} = λ n + sqrt((n+1) - T_n). Then Alice's move: x_{n+2} = λ (n+2) - S_{n+1}. So we have:

T_{n+2} = T_{n+1} + (λ (n+2) - S_{n+1})^2 = (n+1) + (λ (n+2) - S_{n+1})^2.

Now, S_{n+2} = λ (n+2) + y_{n+2}? Actually, after Alice's move, S_{n+2} = λ (n+2) (since she uses full sum). Then Bazza's move: y_{n+2} = sqrt((n+2+1) - T_{n+2}) = sqrt((n+3) - T_{n+2}). So the recurrence becomes:

Define A_n = T_n after Alice's move at odd n. Then:

Let's analyze the recurrence for A_n when both play optimally. The game is a two-player game; we need to find the outcome under optimal play. This is like a "pursuit" game where Alice tries to make A_n ≥ n+1. Bazza tries to keep it below. The threshold λ is such that Alice can force a win if and only if λ > λ*. From the simulations, it seems that for λ ≥ 0.9, she can win; for λ ≤ 0.87, she loses. So λ* is around 0.88? But we need to be precise.

Let's try to derive a necessary and sufficient condition. Consider the game from the start of Alice's turn at an odd n. Suppose she plays x_n = λ n - S (using full sum). Then T = T_old + (λ n - S)^2. Then Bazza will try to either cause overflow or limit future T. The condition for him to win is to have T_old + (λ n - S)^2 < n+1 - 4λ^2? Actually, as we derived, if after Alice's move, T < n+1 - 4λ^2, then he can choose y > 2λ to cause overflow. So for him to be able to cause overflow, we need T < n+1 - 4λ^2. So to prevent that, Alice must ensure T ≥ n+1 - 4λ^2. If she does, he cannot cause immediate overflow. Then he will play y=0, leaving T unchanged. So the recurrence is: At odd n, she adds (λ n - S)^2 to T, and then she wants T to be at least n+1 - 4λ^2. If it is, then after Bazza's move (0), the next odd turn will have the same T? Actually, if he plays 0, then T remains the same, and S remains λ n. Then at the next odd turn n+2, she will play x = λ (n+2) - (λ n) = 2λ, so she adds (2λ)^2 = 4λ^2 to T, and then T becomes T + 4λ^2. So the pattern is: T increases by 4λ^2 every two turns (from odd to odd). The maximum y he can set is sqrt((t+1) - T'), since T' + y^2 ≤ t+1. So if sqrt(t+1 - T') > 2λ, i.e., T' < t+1 - 4λ^2, then he can choose y = sqrt(t+1 - T') (or any y > 2λ) and win. Let's re-read the problem statement: "Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number λ which is known to both players. On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens: If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players. Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy."

So the game is: On odd turns, the constraint is on the sum of x_i, with bound λ n. On even turns, the constraint is on the sum of squares, with bound n. So the players choose numbers to satisfy the constraint. If a player cannot move, the other wins. The game can go on forever if both can always move. So we need to find the set of λ such that Alice can force a win, and those such that Bazza can force a win. Let's try to derive the exact condition analytically. The game can be analyzed by considering the "potential" function f = T - n. Alice wants to make f > 1, Bazza wants to keep f ≤ 1 and also avoid losing. Since the game is symmetric in the sense that the constraints are linear in sum and quadratic in squares, we can attempt to find a "critical" sequence that leads to a draw.

Suppose we look for a sequence where both players play optimally and the game continues forever. That would be a draw. The condition for a draw is that there exists a strategy for Bazza that prevents Alice from ever winning, and also Alice has a strategy to avoid losing (i.e., to keep the game going). Actually, if Bazza can force a win, then he has a strategy to make Alice lose. If Alice can force a win, she has a strategy to make Bazza lose. If the game is a draw, both players have strategies to avoid losing. So the threshold λ* is the boundary where the game switches from Bazza-win to Alice-win. At the threshold, there is a possibility of a draw. So we need to find λ* such that the outcome is a draw, i.e., the game can be played indefinitely.

Thus, we need to find the value of λ for which there exists a strategy for Bazza to keep the game going forever, and also a strategy for Alice to avoid losing. Typically, this is the case when the "critical" λ satisfies a certain equation derived from the recurrence.

From the recurrence we had for A_n (after Alice's move at odd n) under the assumption that Bazza always sets y to make T = n+1 (the maximum allowed), the sequence is given by:

A_{2k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k-1}))^2.

This is a nonlinear recurrence. However, if the game is drawn, we might have that the sequence converges to some pattern where the difference A_n - n tends to a constant. Alternatively, we can look for a fixed point of the "map" from the state after her move to the next state after her move. Since the recurrence is derived from the optimal play (assuming Bazza plays the maximum y), we can try to find λ such that this recurrence yields a constant A_n = n + c for some c? Define n odd: Let A_n be T after Alice's move at turn n. Then after Bazza's move, T = n+1. Then Alice's next move: she sets x_{n+2} = λ(n+2) - S_{n+1}. Thus, we have the recurrence:

A_{n+2} = n+1 + (2λ - sqrt(n+1 - A_n))^2.

This is a numerical problem. But the problem is from an olympiad, so there should be an exact analytic threshold, perhaps λ = √(2) or something like that. Let's try to derive the condition analytically.

We have the recurrence:

A_{2k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k-1}))^2.

Let’s denote B_k = A_{2k+1} - (2k+1). Then we have:

B_k = 1 + (2λ - sqrt(1 - B_{k-1}))^2? Wait, careful: A_{2k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k-1}))^2. But A_{2k-1} = (2k-1) + B_{k-1}. So 2k+2 - A_{2k-1} = 2k+2 - (2k-1 + B_{k-1}) = 3 - B_{k-1}. So sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k-1}) = sqrt(3 - B_{k-1}). Then A_{2k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(3 - B_{k-1}))^2. Then B_k = A_{2k+1} - (2k+1) = 1 + (2λ - sqrt(3 - B_{k-1}))^2. So the recurrence for B_k is:

B_k = 1 + (2λ - sqrt(3 - B_{k-1}))^2.

We also have the initial B_0: For k=0, A_1 = x1^2. With x1=0, A1=0, so B0 = A1 - 1 = -1? Because A1 - 1 = -1. So B0 = -1. But then the recurrence would give B1 = 1 + (2λ - sqrt(3 - (-1)))^2 = 1 + (2λ - sqrt(4))^2 = 1 + (2λ - 2)^2 = 1 + 4(λ-1)^2. For λ=0.912, that gives B1 = 1 + 4*(0.088)^2 = 1 + 0.031 = 1.031, but we know A3 - 3 = 3.747-3=0.747, not 1.031. So the recurrence is not valid for k=1 because we need to include the initial move correctly. Actually, the recurrence we derived for B_k assumes that the formula holds for n≥3. So for k=1, we have A3 = 2 + (3λ - sqrt(2))^2, and then B1 = A3 - 3 = 2 + (3λ - √2)^2 - 3 = (3λ - √2)^2 - 1. So B1 = (3λ - √2)^2 - 1. That is different from the recurrence we wrote. So we need to define the recurrence for B_k properly starting from k=1. Let's derive from scratch.

Let’s index odd turns: t = 2k+1 for k≥0. Let’s define the state at the start of turn t (before Alice's move). After Alice's move, we have T = A_{2k+1}. Then Bazza's move: he sets y = sqrt( (2k+2) - A_{2k+1} ) if A_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2, else he loses. So after his move, T = 2k+2. Then Alice's next move at turn 2k+3: she sets x = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2}. But S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + y. So x = λ(2k+3) - λ(2k+1) - y = 2λ - y. So A_{2k+3} = T_{2k+3} = (2k+2) + (2λ - y)^2 = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k+1}))^2. So the recurrence is:

A_{2k+3} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k+1}))^2.

Thus, for k≥0, we have:

A_{2k+3} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k+1}))^2.

Let n = 2k+1 (odd). After Alice's move, A_{2k+1} = (2k+1) + B_k. Then Bazza's move: y = sqrt( (2k+2) - A_{2k+1} ) = sqrt( 2k+2 - (2k+1) - B_k ) = sqrt(1 - B_k). So after his move, T = 2k+2. Let's try to find strategies.

First, note that both players can always choose x_n = 0, provided that the constraint with x_n = 0 is satisfied. So if at some turn, the cumulative sum (or sum of squares) is already below the bound, the player can always choose 0 and continue. So the only way the game ends is if at a turn, the cumulative sum before the move is already above the bound, making it impossible to choose any nonnegative number. So essentially, the game continues as long as S_n ≤ λ n for odd n, and T_n ≤ n for even n. If a player violates the constraint, they lose immediately. So the players must choose numbers so that the constraints remain satisfied.

Thus we can view the game as: Alice wants to eventually force that at some even n, T_{n-1} > n, so Bazza cannot move. She can influence T_{n-1} via her choices, but note that T_n is cumulative sum of squares, and Bazza also contributes to T_n when he chooses x_n. Alice can try to increase T_{n-1} by choosing large x_n, but she must respect S_n ≤ λ n. So there is a trade-off: increasing x_n increases sum and sum of squares. She can also choose small numbers to keep sums low, but then she might not be able to force a win.

We need to determine the exact set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. Let's try to analyze the game more systematically.

Define the game as an infinite game. The players choose numbers with constraints. The game ends when a player cannot move. The problem asks: For which λ does Alice have a winning strategy? That is, she can force a win. For which λ does Bazza have a winning strategy?

We can think of it as a "positional" game. The state can be represented by the current sum S and sum of squares T, and whose turn it is. Since the constraints depend on parity, the state also includes the index n (which is determined by the number of moves made). However, n is known to both players. So the state is (n, S, T). The players must choose x≥0 such that S+x ≤ λ n (if n odd) or T+y^2 ≤ n (if n even). The game ends if no such x exists.

We want to know for which λ the first player (Alice) has a winning strategy.

Let's denote the set of positions where the player to move can force a win. This is a typical impartial game? Not exactly impartial because the constraints differ based on parity, but the game is two-player with alternating moves. We can attempt to analyze by considering the "critical" conditions.

We can try to find a strategy for Bazza for λ small. For λ < √2/3, we already have a winning strategy: he can force S2 > 3λ, causing Alice to lose at turn3. For λ = √2/3, we have S2 = √2 ≈1.414, and 3λ=√2, so S2=1.414 = 3λ, so she can set x3=0, survive. Then the game continues. But can Bazza still force a win later? Possibly for λ up to some threshold, he can still win by a different method.

Let's analyze for λ between √2/3 and 1/√2. We need to see if Alice can win. For λ=0.7, we saw that if she plays x1=0 and then uses S_n = λ n, she doesn't win. But maybe she can choose a different first move, like x1=0.2, or something, to eventually build squares faster. She might also choose to not set S_n = λ n on all odd turns; she could set some x_n to be larger than the sum bound? No, she cannot exceed λ n. So the maximum x she can add on her turn is λ n - S_{n-1}. To maximize squares, she wants x as large as possible, so she should set S_n = λ n. But if Bazza adds squares, that increases T, which helps her, but he will set y=0 to keep T low. So the only way to increase T is on her turns. So she wants to maximize the sum of squares she adds. She can also try to "save up" sum by playing 0 on some odd turns to let Bazza add squares? But Bazza will not add squares; he will set y=0. So that doesn't help. She could try to make the sum S as small as possible to allow larger x on later turns? Actually, if she keeps sum small, then on her turn, x = λ n - S_{n-1} is larger, so she adds more squares. So it's beneficial to keep S small. That suggests she should set x1=0 to keep sum low. Then on turn3, she can set x3 = 3λ - S2, where S2 = y (Bazza's move). To maximize x3, she would want S2 as small as possible, so she wants Bazza to choose y=0. But he won't; he will choose y to minimize her chance. So she has to adapt.

In the simulation for λ=0.7, we saw that if she sets x1=0, then Bazza can set y=1.05, making T3=2.205, and then she continues with x5=1.4, T5=4.165, etc., and never exceeds n+1. Let's see if she can force a win by setting x1 such that after turn3, T3 > 4. For that, we need T3 = T2 + (3λ - (x1+y))^2 > 4, with T2 = x1^2+y^2, and y≥0, x1≥0, y^2 ≤ 2 - x1^2. So T3 = x1^2+y^2+(3λ - x1 - y)^2. For a given λ, can she choose x1 to guarantee that no matter what y Bazza chooses (subject to y^2 ≤ 2-x1^2), T3 > 4? If she can, she wins on turn4. This is a condition for a winning strategy on the second move.

It seems that for λ=0.72, Bazza can always force a win by making S at the start of her turn exceed λ n. Let's try to find if there is any λ for which she can avoid that. The condition for her to survive at turn n (odd) is that S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. After her move at turn n-2 (odd), we have S_{n-2}=λ (n-2). Then Bazza's turn (n-1 even), he chooses y_{n-1} with y_{n-1}^2 ≤ (n-1) - T_{n-2} (since T_{n-1} = T_{n-2}+y_{n-1}^2 ≤ n-1). He can increase S_{n-1} by y_{n-1}. To cause her loss at turn n, he needs S_{n-1} > λ n, i.e., λ (n-2) + y_{n-1} > λ n => y_{n-1} > 2λ. So if he can choose y_{n-1} > 2λ, then she loses. But he must also have y_{n-1}^2 ≤ (n-1) - T_{n-2}. So if (n-1) - T_{n-2} is large enough to allow y_{n-1} > 2λ, then he can. So the question reduces to: Can Bazza, at some even turn, make his y large enough to exceed 2λ, given the state? If he can, he wins. If not, Alice can continue.

Thus, Alice wants to ensure that for all even n, the maximum possible y she can play (given T_{n-1}) is ≤ 2λ, so that Bazza cannot increase sum beyond λ n. She can influence T_{n-1} by her choices. So she might try to keep T_{n-1} large enough that the bound (n-1) - T_{n-1} is small, limiting y. Actually, the maximum y is sqrt((n-1) - T_{n-1})? Wait, on turn n (even), the constraint is T_n ≤ n, so T_{n-1} + y^2 ≤ n => y ≤ sqrt(n - T_{n-1}). So if T_{n-1} is close to n, then y is small. So if she can make T_{n-1} large, she limits his ability to increase sum. So she wants to maximize T quickly, but she also must respect sum bounds. However, if she tries to increase T, she might be limited by sum. So the game is about the trade-off.

This is reminiscent of a "balancing" game where the threshold λ is such that if λ > something, she can force T to be large enough to prevent him from causing sum overflow; if λ is smaller, he can cause overflow.

Let's try to find the exact critical λ by analyzing the game. The game seems to be about whether Alice can "save up" enough squares to eventually exceed the index. The condition for her to win is that there exists a sequence of moves such that she can force T_n > n+1 for some odd n. The key observation: The game can be analyzed by considering the "balance" condition that if λ is large enough, Alice can force the sum of squares to grow faster than the index, and Bazza cannot prevent it because he cannot increase the sum enough to cause her loss. Actually, he wants to make it harder for her to later push T over the index. But his choice also affects S, which affects her ability to add squares. Since T is the sum of squares, and she wants it to be large, his increasing y increases T' (since y^2 adds to T). That would help her, so he will choose y=0 to keep T' as small as possible. But could setting y>0 ever help him? It might increase S, making her next x smaller, potentially reducing future squares. However, it also increases T, which is bad for him. So the trade-off is: if he sets y>0, he increases T (which is bad) and increases S (which may limit her future x). So the optimal for him is to set y=0 unless setting y>0 causes her to be unable to move on her next turn, which would be a win for him. So if at some even turn, he can choose y such that S_n + x + y > λ (n+2) (i.e., she cannot move at her next turn), he wins. So he will try to make S large enough to cause that.

Rearranged: (4k+5)λ^2 + 4λ^2 < 2k+2 => (4k+9)λ^2 < 2k+2. For large k, the left side grows as 4kλ^2, right side as 2k, so if λ^2 > 0.5, i.e., λ > 1/√2, then eventually the inequality fails (since left side grows faster). Let's re-read the problem statement: "Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number λ which is known to both players. On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens: If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players. Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy."

So the game is: On odd turns, the constraint is on the sum of x_i, with bound λ n. On even turns, the constraint is on the sum of squares, with bound n. Let's try to derive the exact condition analytically. The game can be analyzed by considering the "potential" function f = T - n. Alice wants to make f > 1, Bazza wants to keep f ≤ 1 and also avoid losing. Since the game is symmetric in the sense that the constraints are linear in sum and quadratic in squares, we can attempt to find a "critical" sequence that leads to a draw.

Suppose we look for a sequence where both players play optimally and the game continues forever. That would be a draw. The condition for a draw is that there exists a strategy for Bazza that prevents Alice from ever winning, and also Alice has a strategy to avoid losing (i.e., to keep the game going). Actually, if Bazza can force a win, then he has a strategy to make Alice lose. If Alice can force a win, she has a strategy to make Bazza lose. If the game is a draw, both players have strategies to avoid losing. So the threshold λ* is the boundary where the game switches from Bazza-win to Alice-win. At the threshold, there is a possibility of a draw. So we need to find λ* such that the outcome is a draw, i.e., the game can be played indefinitely.

Thus, we need to find the value of λ for which there exists a strategy for Bazza to keep the game going forever, and also a strategy for Alice to avoid losing. Typically, this is the case when the "critical" λ satisfies a certain equation derived from the recurrence.

From the recurrence we had for A_n (after Alice's move at odd n) under the assumption that Bazza always sets y to make T = n+1 (the maximum allowed), the sequence is given by:

A_{2k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - A_{2k-1}))^2.

This is a nonlinear recurrence. However, if the game is drawn, we might have that the sequence converges to some pattern where the difference A_n - n tends to a constant. Alternatively, we can look for a fixed point of the "map" from the state after her move to the next state after her move. Define n odd: Let A_n be T after Alice's move at turn n. Then after Bazza's move, T = n+1. Then Alice's next move: she sets x_{n+2} = λ(n+2) - S_{n+1}. But S_{n+1} = λ n + y_n, where y_n = sqrt(n+1 - A_n). So x_{n+2} = λ(n+2) - (λ n + y_n) = 2λ - y_n. So A_{n+2} = (n+1) + (2λ - y_n)^2 = (n+1) + (2λ - sqrt(n+1 - A_n))^2.

Thus, we have the recurrence:

A_{n+2} = n+1 + (2λ - sqrt(n+1 - A_n))^2.

On odd turns, Alice chooses x_n such that x_1+...+x_n ≤ λ n. So the players are choosing numbers to satisfy the cumulative constraints. The game ends when a player cannot move. So if Alice makes the sum of squares exceed n at her turn, then Bazza cannot move because his constraint would be violated. But note: Bazza's constraint is on the sum of squares, and he must have T_n ≤ n. So if after Alice's move, T_n > n, then Bazza's turn, he must choose x_n such that T_{n-1}+x_n^2 ≤ n. But if T_{n-1} already > n, then he cannot move. However, after Alice's move, the index is n (odd). Then Bazza's turn is n+1 (even). So the condition for Bazza to be unable to move is that T_n > n+1? Actually, his bound is n+1: he must have T_n + x_{n+1}^2 ≤ n+1. If T_n > n+1, then he cannot move. So Alice can win by making T_n > n+1. That is the condition we used.

Thus, the threshold is where Alice can force T_n > n+1 for some n. This is similar to a "race" where she tries to increase the sum of squares faster than the index.

A_{2k+1} = (2k+1) + B_k.

We want to know if there exists some k such that B_k > 1? Because if B_k > 1, then A_{2k+1} > 2k+2, so T > n+1 at odd turn, and Alice wins. If B_k ≤ 1 for all k, then Bazza can keep the game going. Since B_k is defined by a recurrence that is a function of λ, we can analyze the condition for the sequence to stay ≤ 1.

Let's denote the map f(b) = (2λ - sqrt(1-b))^2 - 1. This is a function defined for b ≤ 1. For a given λ, the sequence B_{k+1} = f(B_k) starting from B_1. We want to know if there is any b > 1 that can be reached. The map f(b) is increasing in b? Let's compute derivative: f'(b) = 2(2λ - sqrt(1-b)) * (1/(2 sqrt(1-b))) = (2λ - sqrt(1-b))/ sqrt(1-b). Since sqrt(1-b) >0, the sign depends on 2λ - sqrt(1-b). For b small, sqrt(1-b) is close to 1, so if 2λ > 1, then 2λ - sqrt(1-b) is positive for b small enough, so f'(b) > 0. For b larger, sqrt(1-b) decreases, so 2λ - sqrt(1-b) becomes larger, so f'(b) > 0 as long as 2λ > sqrt(1-b). Since sqrt(1-b) ≤ 1, if 2λ ≥ 1, then f'(b) > 0 for all b ≤ 1. So f is increasing.

Now, we can look for fixed points of the map. A fixed point b satisfies b = (2λ - sqrt(1-b))^2 - 1. This is equivalent to b + 1 = (2λ - sqrt(1-b))^2. Since the left side is ≥0, we have 2λ - sqrt(1-b) = ± sqrt(b+1). Since sqrt(1-b) ≥ 0, we need to consider the sign. For b small, sqrt(b+1) is close to 1. If 2λ is close to 1, then the expression may be positive. Typically, we take the positive square root because 2λ - sqrt(1-b) could be positive. So we have 2λ - sqrt(1-b) = sqrt(b+1). Rearranged: 2λ = sqrt(1-b) + sqrt(b+1). So the fixed point b satisfies that equation. This is the same as the condition for the limit of the sequence. Actually, 1.3224^2=1.749, minus1=0.749. So B1=0.749. Fixed point: 1.8244 = sqrt(1-b)+ sqrt(b+1) => square: 3.3287 = 2+2 sqrt(1-b^2) => sqrt(1-b^2)=0.66435 => 1-b^2=0.4412 => b^2=0.5588 => b=0.7476. So fixed point 0.7476, B1=0.749 > 0.7476. So the threshold is where B1 equals fixed point. So we need to solve B1 = b. This is a single equation in λ.
